<div id="review_2106361132" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2106361132" />
    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48502194-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447215825p2/48502194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2106361132?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/48502194-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/23700756-zorba-the-greek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=inspiration">inspiration</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=2017-challenges">2017-challenges</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=philosophical">philosophical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=2017-classic-bingo">2017-classic-bingo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2106361132" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12786044550760814102">This story is magic. Zorba is magic. <br/><br/><i>“He speaks and the world grows bigger. Occasionally, when words no longer suffice, he leaps up and dances. And when dancing no longer suffices he places his santuri on his knees and plays.”</i><br/><br/>I discovered Zorba’s magic when watching the movie—for maybe the second or third time—after a particularly sad event in my life. It helped. It helped like nothing else helped.<br/><br/><i>“I felt, as I listened to Zorba, that the world was recovering its pristine freshness. All the dull</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12786044550760814102" style="display:none">This story is magic. Zorba is magic. <br /><br /><i>“He speaks and the world grows bigger. Occasionally, when words no longer suffice, he leaps up and dances. And when dancing no longer suffices he places his santuri on his knees and plays.”</i><br /><br />I discovered Zorba’s magic when watching the movie—for maybe the second or third time—after a particularly sad event in my life. It helped. It helped like nothing else helped.<br /><br /><i>“I felt, as I listened to Zorba, that the world was recovering its pristine freshness. All the dulled daily things regained the brightness they had in the beginning, when we came out of the hands of God.”</i><br /><br />When I think of Zorba, I’m not as afraid of failure. I’m not as broken-hearted over my sorrows. I’m more hopeful, stronger, tougher. He’s the embodiment of groundedness. He reminds you of how delicious life is, like when you’re in love and all of that wonder and expectation overrides anything negative.<br /><br />The book and the movie are both, I would say, necessary. With the movie you have Greece, in all of its glorious black and white beauty. You have the music. You have Anthony Quinn. But the book is a masterpiece, and with the book you understand the philosophy and get a better idea of how to be more like Zorba.<br /><br /><i>“Because you’re like me, too, only you don’t know it.”</i><br /><br /><i>“Yes, you understand with your brain. You say: ‘This is right, and that’s wrong; this is true, and that isn’t; he’s right, the other one’s wrong …’ But where does that lead us? While you are talking I watch your arms and chest. Well, what are they doing? They’re silent. They don’t say a word. As though they hadn’t a drop of blood between them. Well, what do you think you understand with? With your head? Bah!”</i><br /><br />This isn’t just a book—it’s an experience. It’s a tonic. It is life. Bravo Zorba. Bravo Nikos Kazantzakis. And thank you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12786044550760814102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_203853434" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203853434" />
    <a title="Gussie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5279344-gussie"><img alt="Gussie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430401478p2/5279344.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203853434?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gussie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gussie" href="/user/show/5279344-gussie">Gussie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571177073" href="/book/show/995948.Zorba_The_Greek">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">pretentious windbags with a penchant for steaming pieces of pseudo crap</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203853434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13162642771417186870">The only reason why I did not fling this book out of the window was because of a philosophy that I had just picked up from a new English teacher who was my latest "wanna-be-like-her-when-I-grow-up" idol. I had acquired this noble idea of of giving everything at least one chance. I gave it to <i>Zorba the Greek</i> as well and am I glad I did! I would not have been able to shred it apart so easily had I abandoned this utter crapfest midway. <br/><br/>I shouldn't blame Nikos Kazan-whatever with the unpronounceable</span>
  <span id="freeText13162642771417186870" style="display:none">The only reason why I did not fling this book out of the window was because of a philosophy that I had just picked up from a new English teacher who was my latest "wanna-be-like-her-when-I-grow-up" idol. I had acquired this noble idea of of giving everything at least one chance. I gave it to <i>Zorba the Greek</i> as well and am I glad I did! I would not have been able to shred it apart so easily had I abandoned this utter crapfest midway. <br /><br />I shouldn't blame Nikos Kazan-whatever with the unpronounceable last name for making my blood boil because in reality, it's YOU who are to blame. Yes, you there crouching in the corner wearing loose "intellectual type" clothes and carrying a wannabe <i>jhola</i> (or whatever the Western equivalent of that may be). And you too, smoking rolled up cigarettes and droning on in your pant-crappingly boring monotone about some "profound" work that you recently read. In a more general sense of Goodreads voters and reviewers, you are presumably a sad constituent too. As of this moment, <i>Zorba the Greek</i> has an average rating of 4.03 stars out of five on the basis of 4,018 ratings. Like many other averages, it is complete nonsense. <br /><br />I am fighting the insidious cabal of 'respectable' opinion. I offer my head to the enraged rabble fed on pretentious prose in order to warn you what an absolute dungbomb <i>Zorba the Greek</i> is. Kazantzakis, I suspect, was an author who was better suited to something more prosaic - like fumigation.<br /><br />After reading great things about this book and its movie adaptation, one would have thought that it would turn out to be something absolutely life-altering. Instead, you just wade through a long piece of garbage which goes on and on about a sad, clumsy dance between a sensualist crapbag and an over-educated Buddhist wannabe. Zorba's views on life are presented as something that are the equivalent of what Aristotle may have spewed out at Lyceum. The truth is that even an utterly phony Rahul Bose movie is more tolerable that Zorba's pearls of wisdom.<br /><br />Anyhoo, here are a few of the Zorba-esque moral science texbook lessons that this book imparts:<br /><br />-<i>All women LOVE sex, anytime and at any place. A man just has to go and grope a woman and she will instantly pleasure him.</i><br />Bravo. I bet that's a part of the first chapter in 'The Pervert's Guide to A Successful Rape'.<br /><br />-<i>Zorba, the philosopher thinks that books are sheer nonsense. Real beauty is out there in the world.</i><br />Very profound. Jagjit Singh must have taken inspiration for <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jMfWi2kE898" rel="nofollow noopener">this ghazal</a> from Zorba, as would have every darn go-out-and-have-fun-kids kind of book on this planet.<br /><br />-<i>A sodomite monk kills his pretty young toyboy. Zorba tells the narrator that this is the way of the world and the narrator experiences wisdom.</i><br />Boo-hoo at the murder, and applause at the absolutely unique lesson of life. As if we didn't learn what the way of the world was the moment the fat kid stole our lunch in kindergarten and got away with it.<br /><br />-<i>The pretentious navel-gazer narrator realises in the end that life is not all about constant thinking and writing, but it can be full of enjoyment. </i><br />We definitely needed to read that. After all, someone who spends their days reading this garbage DOES need to go out and enjoy life a little - preferably in the company of rowdy school dropouts or something.<br /><br />-<i>The spineless narrator also learns that when he covets something, he should try and make it his (without breaking the rules, of course).</i> <br />Same pinch, Zorba! My mommy taught me all that on her knee. Want the chocolate chip cookies, kid? Try to jump and reach the top shelf of the kitchen (without using a chair to stand on, of course).<br /><br />-<i>Zorba, on the other hand, seems to have understood that his actions and his words have an impact on the people around him as in the case of Madame Hortense and that he really ought to think twice when it comes to playing with the feelings of others. </i><br />And to reach THAT enlightening conclusion, you have to wade through a <br />stinking pile of endlessly monotonous prose. <br /><br /><br />My main gripe with this work is not with the idea or the BFFs kind of pair of Zorba and Buddha Wannabe. It is just the shoddy prose which fails to engage the reader and simply drifts off into meaningless mumbo-jumbo with a smattering of a few aphorisms. The straw which broke the <i>etc.</i> came when Dame Hortense conks off and Zorba grieves like a motherless fawn while the Buddha Wannabe narrator goes into a philosophy churning overdrive about life's evanescence and yada yada. Good GRIEF. I've read better carpe diem type of stuff even in otherwise utter faeces like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/105578.One_Night_at_the_Call_Center" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>One Night @ The Call Center</i>
</a>. <br /><br />There is also a lot of chauvinism, sexism, and misogyny. There is an incident describing the beheading of a woman for rejecting a man’s advances. There is an old woman's character who had been ill-used by men all her life and who Zorba basically treats like a bootycall (for the lack of a better word). However, I am not going to complain about all that. Women are not exactly depicted in the best of light but that is how their situation in Greece (or in many parts of the world, for that matter) was in the 1940s. It can get extremely offensive but let us give poor Kazantzakis his due. He can't be lynched for portraying reality. <br /><br /><br />The main reason why I gave this book two stars instead of one is simply because I like the pretty picture of the physical beauty of Crete that the author paints. I want to visit the island now and forget that I ever read a terribly pseudo book about it. <br /><br /><br />If one looks only at the superficial message that Zorba’s character espouses, then the book is fine (but still not very enjoyable). However, if one looks at how he actually lives his life and how he treats people, then there is a problem. He has no qualms about using those around him to further his own wants, be it using the narrator for his money, or Madame Hortense (or that girl he had an affair with in that other town) for his own benefit. I can agree to the "live life to the fullest" maxim and many other similar ideas that he supports but I just find them unpalatable coming from an absolute little turd like him. <br /><br />As for the much-romanticised "passionate" life in the book, I'm sure it may have helped many terribly depressed people at some point in their drowning-sorrows-in-a-bottle kind of lives. However, as a stand-alone philosophy, I see it as something totally dishonest that fails to take account of one of the most important things in our lives: human relationships and the importance of looking after each other, or if not that, then at least not trampling over every darn human being in your quest to live a "passionate" life. <br /><br /><br />There isn't much about living life in this so-called wonderful piece of literature that I couldn't have learnt from reading some phony self-help book or watching a couple of spiritual 'Art of Living' kind of videos. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13162642771417186870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_61268596" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61268596" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61268596?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=i-saw-the-movie-too">i-saw-the-movie-too</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61268596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6627181925980084328"><br/><i>When everything goes wrong, what a joy to test your soul and see if it has endurance and courage! An invisible and all-powerful enemy - some call him God, others the Devil, seems to rush upon us to destroy us; but we are not destroyed .</i><br/><br/>Story set in Cretan scenery – imagine raw landscape, composed of granite and limestone ; silvery olives, figs and vine plants, orange groves, scent of sage and mint, the vast expanse of the sea and an emerald islands in the distance, bathed in sunlight. There’s no</span>
  <span id="freeText6627181925980084328" style="display:none"><br><i>When everything goes wrong, what a joy to test your soul and see if it has endurance and courage! An invisible and all-powerful enemy - some call him God, others the Devil, seems to rush upon us to destroy us; but we are not destroyed .</i><br><br>Story set in Cretan scenery – imagine raw landscape, composed of granite and limestone ; silvery olives, figs and vine plants, orange groves, scent of sage and mint, the vast expanse of the sea and an emerald islands in the distance, bathed in sunlight. There’s no other place where time flows so lazy. Greece, the cradle of civilization.<br> <br>Narrator, a bookworm who craves for real life, arrives here to manage a coal mine. Earlier in the port cafe he meets Zorba who offers him services. Men are different in almost everything: the origin, age, beliefs, temperament, religion, way of life. But so begins their friendship.<br><br>Zorba is one of the most life-affirming character in all literature I know. When we meet him he's a 65-year-old but young at heart man, who travelled far and wide, yearning for women, wine and life. On the other hand, he's a simple and sensitive person, able to admire the beauty of nature, who hides deeply own worries and anguish and is enjoying life. A man with zest for life, with his own philosophy of life "I act as if I was going to die at any moment” Banal ? What of it ?<br><br>This book is neither for telling nor reviewing. This book is for drinking and eating, for singing and dancing, for laughing and breathing. This book is for you could say when you’ve failed <i> That's nothing! Nothing to worry about </i>. This book is for you could on your death bed shout <i> I've done heaps and heaps of things in my life, but I still did not do enough. Men like me ought to live a thousand years. Good night!</i>. This book is for Life. <br><br>Teach me to dance , Zorba.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1496229360i/22891863._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="6627181925980084328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_470556347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/470556347" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2447803-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246308632p2/2447803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/470556347?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/2447803-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671211325" href="/book/show/936815.Zorba_the_Greek">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer470556347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18196068291004698270">Don’t read this book either. I nabbed it at the library because of the first 2 sentences. I lived on Crete, Greece for a year, on the rural bluffs over Kalathas Beach on the island’s northwest spine. I was hoping to reminisce about the countryside, the pebble beaches, the goats, the nudity, the olive and grape fields, the odors, and the blooming bougainvillea that climbed the outdoor plumbing and entered my second story bathroom window.<br/><br/>I wanted to be your expert and validate the setting of <i>Zorba</i></span>
  <span id="freeText18196068291004698270" style="display:none">Don’t read this book either. I nabbed it at the library because of the first 2 sentences. I lived on Crete, Greece for a year, on the rural bluffs over Kalathas Beach on the island’s northwest spine. I was hoping to reminisce about the countryside, the pebble beaches, the goats, the nudity, the olive and grape fields, the odors, and the blooming bougainvillea that climbed the outdoor plumbing and entered my second story bathroom window.<br /><br />I wanted to be your expert and validate the setting of <i>Zorba the Greek</i>, which, except for more internal combustion engines today, should be unchanged since it was written in the 1940’s. Instead the book was written to capture the spirit and culture of the people, not to document the environs. I was nostalgic for the beauty of the dry, mountainous island at the foot of Greece and the darkened nipples of topless sunbathers. So there’s no Samaria Gorge, no Eliphanesis Beach, no cobalt blue of the Libyan Sea, no Palace of Knossos, no beach caves of Matala, no shallow Roman-era harbors of Rethymno or Chania. <br /><br />I would live again in Crete. No wi-fi, no Western news cycle, no problem.<br /><br /><i>Zorba the Greek</i> is the oft-repeated story of 2 men at odds about how to fully enjoy and experience life. Zorba lives by action; the narrator lives by study and writing (a thinly veiled biographic for Kazantzakis himself?). Isolated together for several months in a small town on Crete, we see the juxtaposition. The allegory is not overly heavy-handed, but it’s apparent from the outset and lacks discriminating subtlety. The narrator begins to revere Zorba, this little dancing man, and by the end has somewhat incorporated his worldview, and leaves the reader to believe he will adopt the zany spontaneity and overly machismo characteristics Zorba exhibits like a male bird in the bough.<br /> <br />These manly stories of vicarious self discovery are better written by the Russians.<br /><br />The only thing I found downright distasteful about the story is a tenor of misogyny throughout, a sure product of the caucasian, Mediterranean male (something that also hasn’t changed since the ‘40s). Women are objects to sleep with and figures that prepare food. Whatever, it's periodic writing. It's a forgettable story.<br /><br />Read <i>Zorba</i> if it’s the only book available and you’re laid over in Cincinnati for a Christmas Nor'easter, waiting for a break in weather at a mid-Atlantic city.<br /> <br />No outstanding sentences, but I did learn a new word: <i>bey</i>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18196068291004698270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_231734044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231734044" />
    <a title="Alex is The Romance Fox" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6663962-alex-is-the-romance-fox"><img alt="Alex is The Romance Fox" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428866380p2/6663962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/231734044?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex is The Romance Fox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex is The Romance Fox" href="/user/show/6663962-alex-is-the-romance-fox">Alex is The Romance Fox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/17232910-zorba-the-greek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6663962-alex-is-the-romance-fox?shelf=favorites-books">favorites-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6663962-alex-is-the-romance-fox?shelf=authors-i-o">authors-i-o</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6663962-alex-is-the-romance-fox?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6663962-alex-is-the-romance-fox?shelf=classics-poetry-philosophy">classics-poetry-philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer231734044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3875257307253402010">I remember very clearly when I first read Zorba the Greek, by Nikos Kazantzakis – I was on my first visit to Greece and meandering through the Monastiraki Flea Market in Athens, came across one of the many bookstalls offering a variety of reading material for us tourists! And among all the latest thrillers and travel books…there was one book that seemed to be saying….you have to read me…....<blockquote>
  
    <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112845.jpg' alt=' photo download201_zpsexy2s0qf.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
  
</blockquote> This was a book that took me on an intoxicating journey leading me to my everlasting connecti</span>
  <span id="freeText3875257307253402010" style="display:none">I remember very clearly when I first read Zorba the Greek, by Nikos Kazantzakis – I was on my first visit to Greece and meandering through the Monastiraki Flea Market in Athens, came across one of the many bookstalls offering a variety of reading material for us tourists! And among all the latest thrillers and travel books…there was one book that seemed to be saying….you have to read me…....<blockquote>
  
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112845.jpg" alt=" photo download201_zpsexy2s0qf.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  
</blockquote> This was a book that took me on an intoxicating journey leading me to my everlasting connection to the world of Greece and Greeks. A love for life, for passion, for living each day to the full…. To feel….<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112846.jpg" alt=" photo Quinn-Zorba_zpsi9uaavja.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>And since my first reading then, 1973, I have read it many times again….and each time I am equally inspired and astounded by this beautiful book that did so much more than just take me on that journey!!! <br>It is a book that should be read by anyone who wants to be touched and perhaps be transformed by how we see our lives.<br><br>What is this book about? I read a synopsis that I feel best describes it….<br><br>Synopsis kindly obtained from Wikipedia<br><b>Zorba the Greek resists easy definition. Like the Odyssey and Don Quixote, it is nearly plotless but never pointless. Like the heroes of those fictional sagas, its hero, Alexis Zorba, casts a larger shadow on the world than the world does on him. … <br><br>Who is Zorba? He is Everyman with a Greek accent. He is Sinbad crossed with Sancho Panza. He is the Shavian Life Force poured into a long, lean, fierce-mustached Greek whose 65 years in the Mediterranean sun have neither dimmed his hawk eyes nor dulled his pagan laughter. … Zorba is a great unbeliever in everything but the abundant life. Pockmarked with bullet scars, he has no faith in war. Full of reverent awe before the universe, he cannot stomach organized religion or priests ("[They] even fleece their fleas"). Child of instinct, Zorba defines the hours as if he had created them. "Daytime is a man," he explains, "night is a woman."<br><br>On many a night Zorba heads for the home of Bouboulina, a blowzy, scow-bottomed "old siren," once the darling of admirals and of fleets. When his boss refuses to make love to a young, appetizing widow, Zorba warns him: "Every man has his folly, but the greatest folly of all ... is not to have one." The boss takes Zorba's advice to heart and the young widow to bed. Meanwhile, Zorba never misses a chance to ask such puzzlers as: What is a woman? Who made the stars? Why do men die? The boss's widow is murdered by puritanical peasants, Bouboulina dies, the lignite mine fails — and all these calamities lead to the heart of Zorba's message: live as if one were to die the next minute.</b><br><br>It’s a story of friendship, self-discovery and finding inner-strength. Two people, so different…..Alexis Zorba, philosopher living life to the full <blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112847.png" alt=" photo 0283451e-b0f0-4c22-bcdd-b7c907f0cbb4_zpskdyw6drx.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></blockquote> and the narrator, a quiet, scholarly writer who has come to Greece to re-open a mine in a small Cretan village….<blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112848.jpg" alt=" photo AsSceneOnTV200026_zpsufptu5tj.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></blockquote>As the two men grow closer, Zorba slowly reveals to the narrator the story of his life…….and thus having a profound effect on how much he has missed out in the joy of just living….the exuberance of Zorba is intoxicating and profound and a joy to behold…<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112849.jpg" alt=" photo zorba_dance1_zpsang6j3ul.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>This is a powerful book that will inspire you. How we should live our lives fully and not waste a moment away. Live each day as if it were your last day!!! <blockquote><b> “there are three kinds of men: those who make it their aim, as they say, to live their lives, eat, drink, make love, grow rich, and famous; then come those who make it their aim not to live their own lives but to concern themselves with the lives of all men – they feel that all men are one and they try to enlighten them, to love them as much as they can and do good to them; finally there are those who aim at living the life of the entire universe – everything, men, animals, trees, stars, we are all one, we are all one substance involved in the same terrible struggle. What struggle?…Turning matter into spirit.” </b></blockquote> And yes, I did find my passionate Greek man….my husband Costa, was a true Greek at heart and we shared our lives together in the most glorious way!!!! <blockquote><b>While experiencing happiness, we have difficulty in being conscious of it. Only when the happiness is past and we look back on it we do suddenly realize — sometimes with astonishment — how happy we had been.</b></blockquote>Please…if you have not read this book…..I urge you to do it now!!! You will be forever happy you did.<br><br><b>One of many favorite quotes that I loved in this book :</b><br><blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441465981i/16112850.jpg" alt=" photo unnamed201_zpse9te0t45.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="3875257307253402010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1333703942" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1333703942" />
    <a title="Momina Masood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood"><img alt="Momina Masood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423900868p2/17262906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1333703942?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Momina Masood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Momina Masood" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood">Momina Masood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780684825540" href="/book/show/822361.Zorba_the_Greek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=greek-lit">greek-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1333703942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3466925644168067887"><blockquote>
  <i>“Come on Zorba,” I cried, “teach me to dance!”</i>
</blockquote> <br/><br/>The reading process should never be ahistorical--this is the normative ideal, not exactly what happens. When I pick up a text from a different age which explores the psychological workings of a people unknown to me, I cannot help but understand and judge them through the filter of my own subjectivity--something which in turn I owe to the society and time to which I belong. This book isn’t meant to be misogynist, but if I brush aside Zorba’s nonc</span>
  <span id="freeText3466925644168067887" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“Come on Zorba,” I cried, “teach me to dance!”</i>
</blockquote> <br /><br />The reading process should never be ahistorical--this is the normative ideal, not exactly what happens. When I pick up a text from a different age which explores the psychological workings of a people unknown to me, I cannot help but understand and judge them through the filter of my own subjectivity--something which in turn I owe to the society and time to which I belong. This book isn’t meant to be misogynist, but if I brush aside Zorba’s nonchalant and redundant remarks denigrating women and simply laugh them off, that will be highly dishonest of me. And the fact that he is celebrated in the book as a Nietzschean Übermensch makes it all the more difficult. This book, for the feminist of today, isn’t an easy read at all, for other than Zorba, the entire Cretan landscape in which the book is located, is absolutely abominable and disgusting. It certainly has “literary value” for it is written quite well, and draws its characters perfectly, but it is hard to get through. Zorba’s Dionysian joie de vivre might make him redeemable, as he certainly wasn’t as despicable as his Cretan contemporaries, but I’m not really sure if the woman of today will warm up to him, despite his heroism and grandeur. Most great men in history didn’t really have flattering opinions on women, which certainly puts their greatness and our own standards to question. Anyway, I’m not sure if I’ll recommend this book. The movie, too, was okay but I will say that Anthony Quinn was a perfect Zorba, and I couldn’t help but visualize him during reading. Also props to the lady who played Dame Hortense. I remember she broke my heart whilst watching the movie years ago, and I remember how disillusioned I was with the classic which had promised so much, and had only left a bad taste in the mouth. <br /><br />Well, to each their own I guess.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3466925644168067887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1745092801" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1745092801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12484502500648654386">Not as much a book as a reading experience, Kazantzakis's Zorba is the quintessential novel of a normal person coming into contact with an explosively hilarious bigger-than-life character - Zorba! It is engaging, funny, touching and extremely entertaining. Of course, many have seen the classic film made from it, but seriously, the book is really quite extraordinary. Perhaps the greatest novel among many great books by this under appreciated Greek writer.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_751499018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/751499018" />
    <a title="Chris Shank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2764185-chris-shank"><img alt="Chris Shank" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598475661p2/2764185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/751499018?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Shank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Shank" href="/user/show/2764185-chris-shank">Chris Shank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer751499018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17546030409233062949">Some top 100 lists include Zorba The Greek in the best books of all time. Gimme a break. Before reading it, I had several times stumbled upon quotes from this work, like the following, which encouraged me to read it, : “All those who actually live the mysteries of life haven't the time to write, and all those who have the time don't live them!” Not bad. But the seemingly profound, extrapolated statements are absolutely wrung dry and beggared by a literary and philosophical context as poor as any</span>
  <span id="freeText17546030409233062949" style="display:none">Some top 100 lists include Zorba The Greek in the best books of all time. Gimme a break. Before reading it, I had several times stumbled upon quotes from this work, like the following, which encouraged me to read it, : “All those who actually live the mysteries of life haven't the time to write, and all those who have the time don't live them!” Not bad. But the seemingly profound, extrapolated statements are absolutely wrung dry and beggared by a literary and philosophical context as poor as any I’ve ever read. Zorba is a sensualist, a completely lascivious man able only to offer intuitive observations about the world which came across to me as childish, offhand, and specious. Also, the narrator portrays himself, perhaps unintentionally, as weak-willed and swayed by every insect-like whim of Zorba. He is convinced that Zorba is somehow plugged in deeply to his human ancestral roots and thus closer to the meaning of life than most. I see nothing of the sort. Zorba is honest, passionate, and strong…but not emotionally/ relationally/ intellectually evolved. Reason has, I believe, made possible a better life for mankind, and even if it hasn’t yet been made to work for some, going backward hardly solves our problems. Zorba’s primitive instincts are easy to understand and consistent, but they aren’t forceful or pragmatic in the long run. I understand that the author is attempting to depict a contrast between Zorba and the narrator, between instinct and intellect, but he falls too easily the side of the destitute animal and not the struggling angel in humanity.<br /><br />More specifically, Zorba is an undisciplined skirt-chaser who punches his boss without a thought about consequences, rages about trivial events, is driven by hunger, changes him mind about the world every day, relentlessly pursues old widows to sleep with in his travels, and harasses his friend when he won’t sleep around with new women that he meets. To be frank, I just grew sick of Zorba’s crudeness and never-ending rants about his fondness for seducing, and being seduced by, old women. I can only take so much bruv. <br /><br />Granted, Zorba is interesting, colorful, romantic at times, and a hard worker, but mostly he is a joke. This guy is a Bohemian flop whose libidinous wisdom is simplistic and, if anything from him seems profound, it is quickly mudded over by a raving tantrum or story that clearly identifies him as the idiot some poor village lost. Mostly his cleverness is accidental or it is a primal, instinctual urge transmogrified into something more insightful by the alchemy of the more enlightened narrator. Which reminds me, I did like the narrator’s bash against Buddha, believe it or not, when he referred to Buddha as a person who cleansed himself of a will to live and no longer loved this life. I, personally, think Buddha was more than that within his environment, as he emancipated his Hindu brothers from the tyranny of an old asceticism and helped his followers realize a new form and interpretation of happiness not dependant on material possessions or sensory pleasure. I also think secular Buddhism has a lot to offer Western thinkers, but the author’s jibe against him was well-played nonetheless.<br /><br />I kept waiting on the book to change tracks and allow the narrator to pull away from Zorba’s extreme lustiness and intemperance, but it didn’t happen. Good luck mining the few gems out of this one.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17546030409233062949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_487658065" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/487658065" />
    <a title="Hoda Marmar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655700-hoda-marmar"><img alt="Hoda Marmar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572216889p2/3655700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/487658065?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hoda Marmar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hoda Marmar" href="/user/show/3655700-hoda-marmar">Hoda Marmar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10875052">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=arabic">arabic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=bookoholics">bookoholics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=around-the-world-in-books">around-the-world-in-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer487658065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18172536885413710248">Bookoholics' January 2016's book from Greece: Zorba the Greek by Nikos Kazantzakis<br/><br/>Women, the sea, alcohol, food, and hard work are the priorities of Alexis Zorba's life. He embraces the divine and the demonic in him; he laughs at customs, books, dogmas, patriotism, and the supernatural. Dancing is the way he handles extreme emotions. He needs his drink at night so that his soul can feel free. He is thirsty for love; and the more physical love he attains, the more emotional warmth he looks for. <br/>Z</span>
  <span id="freeText18172536885413710248" style="display:none">Bookoholics' January 2016's book from Greece: Zorba the Greek by Nikos Kazantzakis<br /><br />Women, the sea, alcohol, food, and hard work are the priorities of Alexis Zorba's life. He embraces the divine and the demonic in him; he laughs at customs, books, dogmas, patriotism, and the supernatural. Dancing is the way he handles extreme emotions. He needs his drink at night so that his soul can feel free. He is thirsty for love; and the more physical love he attains, the more emotional warmth he looks for. <br />Zorba is painted as an extraordinary madman or wise man by many. To me, he is a very simple man with lots of simple needs and some sass. He looks for instantaneous rewards and satisfaction. He wants things now, right now. These needs are primitive needs: food, alcohol, sex, and money. He disregards everything that gets in the way of him getting these needs.<br />Now, the narrator is far more interesting. He is really struggling. He reflects a lot before deciding on what to do in order to minimize future regrets. He is torn between becoming a Buddha, and satisfying his primitive bodily needs. So, he is quite the opposite of Alexis. He is complex! And, God I love complex characters! He goes to books, writes all night, relies on intuition, daydreams, and floats around. Alexis' 'erratic' lifestyle and beliefs draw him in like a moth is drawn to the brightness. But is a fire burning so bright, or is it a transcendent light? I think it is both. I think that the narrator wasn't right, and Zorba wasn't right. They're both at extremes. I guess that humans would be happiest if they can satisfy both their primitive and their higher intellectual needs. (Think Maslow's pyramid of needs)<br />The novel was quite enjoyable, especially in the first and last quarters. The middle was a bit redundant. The writing style was good. I especially loved the narrator's reflections and inner reasoning. The author was able to transport me to the shores of Crete in Greece. For that, I am highly grateful. This is an unforgettable duo of characters. I quite enjoyed the movie starring Anthony Quinn.<br />“Every man has his folly, but the greatest folly of all … is not to have one.” <br />Let us enjoy our follies while they last!</span>
  <a data-text-id="18172536885413710248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2399917274329163480">"The Life and Deeds of Alexis Zorba" (as it was originally titled in Greek) does not follow the form of a conventional novel. The plot is simplistic: it is the story of a young idealistic scholar and his friend, cook, and foreman Alexis Zorbas who go to Crete to embark on a lignite mining scheme. The content, however, is at once a compelling snapshot of early 20th-century life in Greece and a profound Socratic discourse on the meaning of love, life, and living. "Zorba" requires patience--one mus</span>
  <span id="freeText2399917274329163480" style="display:none">"The Life and Deeds of Alexis Zorba" (as it was originally titled in Greek) does not follow the form of a conventional novel. The plot is simplistic: it is the story of a young idealistic scholar and his friend, cook, and foreman Alexis Zorbas who go to Crete to embark on a lignite mining scheme. The content, however, is at once a compelling snapshot of early 20th-century life in Greece and a profound Socratic discourse on the meaning of love, life, and living. "Zorba" requires patience--one must understand that its progression resembles an afternoon at a Cretan taverna more than a walk down 5th avenue--but when read with care, it will change your life. <br /> "Boss, everything's simple in the world. How many times must I tell you? So don't go and complicate things!"</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3276503957" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3276503957" />
    <a title="Prickle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57115665-prickle"><img alt="Prickle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528206407p2/57115665.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3276503957?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Prickle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Prickle" href="/user/show/57115665-prickle">Prickle</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781476782812" href="/book/show/22716193-zorba-the-greek">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3276503957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8777561194035934094">Or by its intentionally thinly disguised alternate title: <i>Zarathustra the Dancer</i>. <br/><br/>One asks if everything Hermann Hesse wrote was wrong-headed after all when Kazantzakis writes this on such lithe feet and still leaves time to give the Buddha himself such a clowning and slap on the other cheek that one wonders if he wasn't some fable made up to scare children with. And incredibly, he does the same across the gamut of ideologies from Christianity to nationalism in this book without seeming inwardly</span>
  <span id="freeText8777561194035934094" style="display:none">Or by its intentionally thinly disguised alternate title: <i>Zarathustra the Dancer</i>. <br /><br />One asks if everything Hermann Hesse wrote was wrong-headed after all when Kazantzakis writes this on such lithe feet and still leaves time to give the Buddha himself such a clowning and slap on the other cheek that one wonders if he wasn't some fable made up to scare children with. And incredibly, he does the same across the gamut of ideologies from Christianity to nationalism in this book without seeming inwardly spiteful of any of them at all. <br /><br />What a character it must have taken to have been in Greece in WWII and to leave THIS book as the result. And if the Anglo-sphere has gradually forgotten Kazantzakis with time, with just a cursory glance at the Goodreads reviews it seems the mediterranean and middle-East definitely have not, though chilled Northerners in particular would especially benefit from this book. Despite its overtly Nietzschean and Bergsonian core it reminds me much of the Bible, of Don Quixote, of works in the oral tradition with Leskov as easily a third of the book are the immensely poignant anecdotes and vignettes delivered by characters themselves, of Homer of course, and strangely even of The Magic Mountain, but whereas Mann always rounds off every sentence with a polish Kazantzakis doesn't mince words, and doesn't pull back even when something hits overly sentimental or crude to us jaded moderns; it moves constantly at its own rhythm, and is certainly not afraid to deliver harsh words to the reader like an attack on our senses and sedate way of life behind every paragraph. Zorba even encourages a renegade monk to burn down a Greek monastery and gives him a shave afterwards so he looks like a Catholic. But even so, every chapter literally dances with joy.<br /><br />I wrote a much lengthier review and deleted it, as this work certainly doesn't need it. Suffice it to say I liked this book very much. Here's something of one of the vignettes I mentioned, see if it's to your rhythm and palette:<br /><br /><i>"When the royal steamship arrived all decorated with flags and the cannonade began and Prince George trod Cretan soil, have you ever seen an entire population driven mad all together because they've seen their freedom?... If each person were allowed to pick his own heavenly Paradise according to his taste (that's how it should be; that's the meaning of Paradise), I would say to God: 'Dear God, let my Paradise be a Crete filled with myrtles and flags, and let the moment when Prince George treads Cretan soil last for eternities. That's all I want."<br /><br />"What happened in Crete, Zorba? Keep talking!"<br /><br />"Are we going to keep on saying words? Look here, what I'm telling you is that this world is a mystery and every human being is a great brute—a great brute and a great god. A wicked partisan named Yiorgharos had come down with me from Macedonia, a criminal who'd done unbelievable things, a filthy pig—and he was weeping. 'What are you bawling about, Yiorgharos? Why are you boo-hooing, you pig?' I ask him, my own eyes having turned on the waterworks. But he falls all over me and, yes, starts kissing me and sobbing like a babe in arms. Then this miserly skinflint undoes his leather money belt, empties out into his lap the golden pounds he plundered from the Turks he killed and the homes he violated, and tosses them into the air, handful after handful. Do you understand, Boss? That's what freedom means!"</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8777561194035934094" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2950207605" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2950207605" />
    <a title="robin friedman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman"><img alt="robin friedman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571353109p2/22269025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2950207605?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="robin friedman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="robin friedman" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman">robin friedman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2950207605" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6515842487509161088">Learning To Enjoy Life<br/><br/>I had read "Zorba the Greek" many years ago and was prompted to return to it. It seemed to me that my reading had been too top heavy, intellectualized, and critical. I thought this way as well about the readings and discussions in my book group. And so, I vaguely remembered "Zorba", which I had read in an earlier translation, and thought the book had something important to say.<br/><br/>Kazantsakis' novel is a story about exuberance and enjoying life through activity, including sex, </span>
  <span id="freeText6515842487509161088" style="display:none">Learning To Enjoy Life<br /><br />I had read "Zorba the Greek" many years ago and was prompted to return to it. It seemed to me that my reading had been too top heavy, intellectualized, and critical. I thought this way as well about the readings and discussions in my book group. And so, I vaguely remembered "Zorba", which I had read in an earlier translation, and thought the book had something important to say.<br /><br />Kazantsakis' novel is a story about exuberance and enjoying life through activity, including sex, music, dance, and work. It seems to me that people today shy away from this exuberance and joy, particularly in the current climate, where sex is involved. There is a heavy aura of criticism, seriousness -- oh so serious -- a sense of self-righteousness, and tension in how people approach themselves and others and their community and country, or so it seems to me.<br /><br />Written in 1946, the novel is set in Crete in the years following WW I. The book includes allusions to Greek political affairs, but the reader need not know a great deal about them to follow the story. The book is told in the first person by a nameless narrator, 35, who recounts has friendship with a much older man, Zorba, 65, over what is only a few months. <br /><br />The narrator appears fairly well to do. He is introspective and bookish and carries a copy of Dante with him. His primary intellectual interest is in Buddhism and he is in the middle of writing a lengthy play about the Buddha's life and teachings. The narrator is travelling to Crete to work on an abandoned lignite mine when he meets Zorba. With his enthusiasm and charisma, Zorba quickly finds a place in the narrator's heart, and he is hired to supervise operations at the mine. The mine plays an important if secondary role in the story. <br /><br />The two men sit on the beach, smoke and eat, and share their thoughts on sex, religion, nationalism, war, work, and much more. Zorba becomes involved with an aged, promiscuous French singer, Madam Hortense and eventually the narrator has a brief, ill-fated sexual encounter of his own. The two friends explore the beauties of Crete, share the life of the town, and also become involved with several monasteries and churches.<br /><br />Zorba plays his music on the santouri he carries everywhere, leaps and dances, and sings. He tries to bring his friend out of his bookish, introspective life. Oddly enough, the Buddhism which the narrator studies seems closely related to the life-in-the -present of Zorba, without the intellectualization. Zorba wants to be involved in what he is doing at the moment and to give himself over to it completely, whether it is sex, work, eating, drinking, dance, or music. In one of many passages of discussion, the narrator gets Zorba to describe his earlier efforts at a middle-class middling life with a home, wife, and children. The narrator asks Zorba if he played his santouri to relax and "drown his sorrows". Zorba replies:<br /><br />"Bah, it's pretty obvious that you don't play an instrument! What's this drivel your spouting? At home there are family worries; wife, kids, what are we going to eat, how will we dress, what will become of us. Pure hell! And the santouri needs a clean heart. If my wife says one word too many to me, what sort of heart do you want me to have for playing santouri? If the kids go hungry and whine, just you try playing! The santouri makes you think of nothing but santouri, Get it?"<br /><br />There are many provocative passages and discussions throughout "Zorba the Greek" sometimes combined with activity.<br /><br />The narrator introduces his story of Zorba in a Prologue in which he says that the four people who most influenced him are Homer, Henri Bergson, Nietzsche, and ... Zorba. He explains the influence of the first three figures, and Bergson's philosophy of elan vital seems closest to him. (Zorba almost illustrates it.) The narrator writes that "Zorba taught me to love life and not to fear death," The narrator regrets that he could not have learned more from Zorba. He was unable to follow Zorba's life but had to content himself with writing and preserving Zorba's story. He may be too harsh with himself. Everyone follows their own path, and learns what and when they can.<br /><br /> I was moved to reread this book after many years and to be inspired by Zorba. <br /><br />Robin Friedman</span>
  <a data-text-id="6515842487509161088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1606372559" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1606372559" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49577504-george"><img alt="George" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503505120p2/49577504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1606372559?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/49577504-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=greek-literature-modern">greek-literature-modern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=nikos-kazantzakis">nikos-kazantzakis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=read-in-2018-best">read-in-2018-best</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=audible-library">audible-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=greece">greece</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=contemporary-classics">contemporary-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=read-prior-to-2014">read-prior-to-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49577504-george?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1606372559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8657554710543906454">"Zorba The Greek" by Nikos Kazantzakis is one of the great novels of the 20th Century. Published in 1945, it is loosely based on the friendship and adventures of Kazantzakis and George Zorbas a miner from Greece. <br/><br/>Kazantzakis and Zorbas lived and worked together for three years (1916-1919). Among other things, they met at Mount Athos, worked together on a mining venture, and served together in a humanitarian mission to repatriate tens of thousands of persecuted Greeks. After Zorbas died in 1941, </span>
  <span id="freeText8657554710543906454" style="display:none">"Zorba The Greek" by Nikos Kazantzakis is one of the great novels of the 20th Century. Published in 1945, it is loosely based on the friendship and adventures of Kazantzakis and George Zorbas a miner from Greece. <br><br>Kazantzakis and Zorbas lived and worked together for three years (1916-1919). Among other things, they met at Mount Athos, worked together on a mining venture, and served together in a humanitarian mission to repatriate tens of thousands of persecuted Greeks. After Zorbas died in 1941, Kazantzakis wrote this novel to recount their adventures and friendship, and to express his ideas about philosophy and spirituality. <br><br>The novel is set in the year 1916 on the Greek island of Crete (located in the Mediterranean Ocean between Europe, Asia, and Africa). Kazantzakis's Crete is austere and rugged, both the landscape and the people. <br> <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1517313308p8/3667678.jpg" alt="Kazantzakis" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br><b>Four Main Characters</b> <br>There are four main characters in the novel. <br><br>First, the unnamed narrator (really Kazantzakis) is an educated and cultured man, born in Crete, who has returned to the island to conduct a mining operation. He's an intellectual, "book smart," always sharing his philosophical speculations and full of ideas about how to create a new, morally superior world. But he's mostly impotent in real action. <br> <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1517313328p8/3667679.jpg" alt="George Zorbas" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>Second, is Zorba, an uneducated miner (hired to run the mining operation) and a bit of an irresponsible rogue who has "been around." Early in the story you realize that most of what he says should be taken with a grain of salt. In fact, much of what Zorba says is intended to provoke the narrator (whom he calls "Boss") for whom he is the perfect foil. <br> <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1517313367p8/3667680.jpg" alt="Madame Hortense" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>Third is Madame Hortense, an old French siren, a faded beauty, who owns the small, run-down hotel where Zorba and the Boss lodge. She has never married and has always lived life on her own terms. To hear her tell it, she single-handedly saved the island of Crete from destruction. She is an equal match for Zorba. <br> <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1517313499p8/3667682.jpg" alt="The Widow" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>And finally, there is the unnamed young widow. She is the embodiment of fierceness, a woman who unsettles the villagers. If she doesn't frighten him to death, this unnamed woman might just be the salvation of the unnamed narrator. <br><br>All the characters - expect Zorba and Madam Hortense - are Cretan. Most of the characters in the novel are flawed. Zorba – although admired greatly by the Boss – has many flaws. To some modern readers Zorba may be somewhat unlikeable. Although he may be unlikeable, he seems to be an authentic portrait of a certain type of Greek man living in the year 1916. <br><br>These four characters are really four archetypes: <br>1. The Impotent Intellectual<br>2. The Irresponsible Rogue<br>3. The Faded Beauty<br>4. The Young Widow<br><br>Kazantzakis, like a virtuoso conductor, directs these flawed characters against a backdrop of a Cretan peasant village to create music that conveys timeless truths. <br><br><b>Becoming One with the Universe</b> <br>For Kazantzakis, the goal of human life is to become one with the universe - through work, food, wine, dance, art, and, especially, through love. To give your all to whatever you do - no half measures - to forget everything else at that moment you do something. To live, not in the past, not in the future, but in the present moment. To do and see everything as if it was for the first time. <br> <br>Kazantzakis reveals, I believe, an interpretive key for the entire novel in a passage about a mysterious sculpture by the French sculptor Auguste Rodin: <br> <br>"With my head bowed I ruminated on Zorba's words. And suddenly a distant, snow-bound town came to my mind. I was at an exhibition of Rodin's works and I had stopped to look at an enormous bronze hand, "The Hand of God" whose hand was half closed. And in the palm, an ecstatic man and woman were embracing and struggling..." <br> <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1517313459p8/3667681.jpg" alt="Rodin - The Hand of God" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>For Kazantzakis, this sculpture is a symbol of the entire novel and a symbol of all life. It is in the embracing and struggling, that we can become one with the universe. <br><br><b>The Female of the Species</b> <br>By today’s standards, Zorba’s views about women are certainly unacceptable. So is this book simply a “bromance” novel about an arrogant male chauvinist and an impotent bookworm, a book with outdated views about women? To a certain extent yes. It is, after all, set in a patriarchal village on the island of Crete in the year 1916. In it’s patriarchy, Crete was not alone. Even in countries like the United States, women did not have equal rights or the right to vote in 1916. And yet, below the surface, Kazantzakis presents a more complex view of women and men. <br><br>The relationship between males and females is a theme that runs through the entire novel. The Boss desires a morally superior world that includes equal rights for women and men. Zorba, although open to the idea, thinks that it would never work among the villagers who are set in their ways. <br><br>Zorba regularly uses the phrase "The Female of the Species" which is a reference to the Rudyard Kipling 1911 poem ("the Female of Her Species is more deadly than the Male") and probably also a reference to the 1912 silent film with the same title about a miner who is killed by his wife. For Zorba each woman is a siren – alluring, fascinating, and dangerous."Bouboulina," Zorba's nickname for Madame Hortense, is an allusion to Laskarina Bouboulina, a female Greek naval commander and military hero. As the story unfolds, we become aware that Zorba’s view of women is not so simplistic as first imagined. <br><br>Neither the patriarchal village (which dominates women) nor the local monastery (which completely rejects women) are presented in a very positive light by Kazantzakis. In contrast, both Madame Hortense and the young widow (neither is under male control) are heroic in their own ways. In sum, while the novel is, inescapably, a product of its times, it is, nevertheless, nuanced in its presentation of women. <br><br><b>Turning Matter Into Spirit</b> <br>For Kazantzakis, the goal of human life is to "turn matter into spirit." And to realize that "during the short span of our life we are in the service of something immortal." Each of us is a clump of earth that lives, breaths, works, dances, and struggles for a brief span, and then returns to the earth. Like the ecstatic couple in God's hand, we are to embrace and struggle, to become one with all, to transform matter into spirit. <br><br>Rating: 5 out of 5 stars<br><br>Notes: <br>Audiobook: <br> <br>Narrated by: George Guidall <br>Length: 12 hours and 36 minutes <br>Unabridged <br>Release date: 2011-07-27 <br>Publisher: Recorded Books</span>
  <a data-text-id="8657554710543906454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1721707868" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1721707868" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721707868?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
